     <p>
      <b>
       Nicola Sacco
      </b>
      (April 22, 1891 August 23, 1927) and
      <b>
       Bartolomeo Vanzetti
      </b>
      (June 11, 1888 August 23, 1927) were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_people" title="Italian people">
       Italian
      </a>
      -born
      <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">
       anarchists
      </a>
      who were convicted of murdering a guard and a paymaster during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armed_robbery" title="Armed robbery">
       armed robbery
      </a>
      of the Slater and Morrill Shoe Company in
      <a href="/wiki/Braintree,_Massachusetts" title="Braintree, Massachusetts">
       South Braintree
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">
       Massachusetts
      </a>
      , United States, in 1920 and were
      <a href="/wiki/Electrocution" title="Electrocution">
       electrocuted
      </a>
      seven years later at
      <a href="/wiki/Charlestown_State_Prison" title="Charlestown State Prison">
       Charlestown State Prison
      </a>
      . Both adhered to a version of anarchism that combatted war, violence and oppressive governments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LC_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LC-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LC2_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LC2-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After a few hours' deliberation, the jury found Sacco and Vanzetti guilty of first-degree
      <a href="/wiki/Murder" title="Murder">
       murder
      </a>
      on July 14, 1921. A series of appeals followed, funded largely by a private Sacco and Vanzetti Defense Committee. The appeals were based on recanted testimony, conflicting ballistics evidence, a prejudicial pre-trial statement by the jury foreman, and a confession by an alleged participant in the robbery. All appeals were denied by trial judge
      <a href="/wiki/Webster_Thayer" title="Webster Thayer">
       Webster Thayer
      </a>
      and eventually by the Massachusetts State Supreme Court. By 1925, the case had drawn worldwide attention. As details of the trial and the men's suspected innocence became known, Sacco and Vanzetti became the center of one of the largest
      <a href="/wiki/Cause_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bre" title="Cause clbre">
       causes clbres
      </a>
      in modern history. In 1927, protests on their behalf were held in every major city in North America and Europe, as well as in
      <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">
       Tokyo
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">
       Sydney
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="So Paulo">
       So Paulo
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">
       Rio de Janeiro
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">
       Buenos Aires
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">
       Johannesburg
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Celebrated writers, artists, and academics pleaded for their pardon or for a new trial. Harvard law professor and future Supreme Court justice
      <a href="/wiki/Felix_Frankfurter" title="Felix Frankfurter">
       Felix Frankfurter
      </a>
      argued for their innocence in a widely read
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Monthly" title="Atlantic Monthly">
        Atlantic Monthly
       </a>
      </i>
      article that was later published in book form. Sacco and Vanzetti were sentenced to death in April 1927, accelerating the outcry. Responding to a massive influx of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegrams" title="Telegrams">
       telegrams
      </a>
      urging their pardon, Massachusetts governor
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alvan_Fuller" title="Alvan Fuller">
       Alvan Fuller
      </a>
      appointed a three-man commission to investigate the case. After weeks of secret deliberation, which included interviews with the judge, lawyers, and several witnesses, the commission upheld the verdict. Sacco and Vanzetti were executed via electric chair on August 23, 1927.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytexecution_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytexecution-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Subsequent riots destroyed property in Paris, London, and other cities.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since their deaths, some critics have concluded that the two men were convicted largely because of
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Italianism" title="Anti-Italianism">
       anti-Italian prejudice
      </a>
      and their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">
       anarchist
      </a>
      political beliefs and were therefore
      <a href="/wiki/Wrongful_execution" title="Wrongful execution">
       unjustly executed
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montgomery1960_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Montgomery1960-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young1985_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Young1985-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Investigations of the case continued throughout the 1930s and 1940s. The publication of the men's letters, containing eloquent professions of innocence, intensified belief in their wrongful execution. Additional ballistics tests and incriminating statements by the men's acquaintances have clouded the case. In 1977, Massachusetts Governor
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dukakis" title="Michael Dukakis">
       Michael Dukakis
      </a>
      issued a proclamation that Sacco and Vanzetti had been unfairly tried and convicted and that "any disgrace should be forever removed from their names", but did not proclaim them innocent.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
       Background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sacco_e_Vanzetti.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="238" data-file-width="370" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Sacco_e_Vanzetti.PNG" width="370"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        Sacco (left) and Vanzetti
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Sacco was a shoe-maker born in
      <a href="/wiki/Torremaggiore" title="Torremaggiore">
       Torremaggiore
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Foggia" title="Province of Foggia">
       Province of Foggia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">
       Apulia
      </a>
      region,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">
       Italy
      </a>
      , who emigrated to the United States at the age of seventeen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vanzetti was a
      <a href="/wiki/Fishmonger" title="Fishmonger">
       fishmonger
      </a>
      born in
      <a href="/wiki/Villafalletto" title="Villafalletto">
       Villafalletto
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Cuneo" title="Province of Cuneo">
       Province of Cuneo
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Piedmont" title="Piedmont">
       Piedmont
      </a>
      region, who arrived in the United States at age twenty. Both men left Italy for the U.S. in 1908,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although they did not meet until a 1917 strike.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-truTV_Legacy_of_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-truTV_Legacy_of-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The men were believed to be followers of
      <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Galleani" title="Luigi Galleani">
       Luigi Galleani
      </a>
      , an Italian anarchist who advocated revolutionary violence, including bombing and assassination. Galleani published
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cronaca_Sovversiva" title="Cronaca Sovversiva">
        Cronaca Sovversiva
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Subversive Chronicle
      </i>
      ), a periodical that advocated violent revolution, and an explicit bomb-making manual called
      <i>
       La Salute  in voi!
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Health is in you!
      </i>
      ). At the time, Italian anarchists  in particular the Galleanist group  ranked at the top of the United States government's list of dangerous enemies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since 1914, the Galleanists had been identified as suspects in several violent bombings and assassination attempts, including an attempted mass poisoning.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Publication of
      <i>
       Cronaca Sovversiva
      </i>
      was suppressed in July 1918, and the government deported Galleani and eight of his closest associates on June 24, 1919.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-writing.upenn.edu_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-writing.upenn.edu-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Remaining Galleanists remained active. For three years, perhaps 60 Galleanists waged an intermittent campaign of violence against U.S. politicians, judges, and other federal and local officials, especially those who had supported deportation of alien radicals. Among the dozen of more violent acts was the bombing of
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">
       Attorney General
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/A._Mitchell_Palmer" title="A. Mitchell Palmer">
       A. Mitchell Palmer
      </a>
      's home on June 2, 1919. In that incident, Carlo Valdonoci, a former editor of
      <i>
       Cronaca Sovversiva
      </i>
      and an associate of Sacco and Vanzetti, was killed when the bomb intended for Palmer exploded in Valdonoci's hands. Radical pamphlets entitled "Plain Words" signed "The Anarchist Fighters" were found at the scene of this and several other midnight bombings that night.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-writing.upenn.edu_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-writing.upenn.edu-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Several Galleanist associates were suspected or interrogated about their roles in the bombing incidents. Two days before Sacco and Vanzetti were arrested, a Galleanist named
      <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Salsedo" title="Andrea Salsedo">
       Andrea Salsedo
      </a>
      fell to his death from the US
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">
       Justice Department
      </a>
      's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Bureau of Investigation">
       Bureau of Investigation
      </a>
      (BOI) offices on
      <a href="/wiki/Park_Row_Building" title="Park Row Building">
       15 Park Row
      </a>
      in New York City.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charles_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-charles-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Salsedo had worked in the Canzani Printshop in
      <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">
       Brooklyn
      </a>
      , to where federal agents traced the "Plain Words" leaflet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charles_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-charles-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Roberto Elia, a fellow New York printer and admitted anarchist,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      was later deposed in the inquiry, and testified that Salsedo had committed suicide for fear of betraying the others. He portrayed himself as the 'strong' one who had resisted the police.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to anarchist writer
      <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Tresca" title="Carlo Tresca">
       Carlo Tresca
      </a>
      , Elia changed his story later, stating that Federal agents had thrown Salsedo out the window.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Salsedo's death was a disaster for the Bureau, which had lost a potential court witness and source of information.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Galleanists knew that Salsedo had been held by the Bureau and might have talked to authorities. Rumors swirled in the anarchist community that Salsedo had made important disclosures concerning the bomb plot of June 2. The Galleanist plotters realized that they would have to go underground and dispose of any incriminating evidence. After their arrest, Sacco and Vanzetti were found to have correspondence with several Galleanists; one letter warned Sacco to destroy all mail after reading.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Robbery">
       Robbery
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Robbery">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H%26R_Top_Break.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="700" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/H%26R_Top_Break.jpg/220px-H%26R_Top_Break.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/H%26R_Top_Break.jpg/330px-H%26R_Top_Break.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/H%26R_Top_Break.jpg/440px-H%26R_Top_Break.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H%26R_Top_Break.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Harrington and Richardson top break revolver-similar to pistol carried by Berardelli
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      At the Slater-Morrill Shoe Company factory, on Pearl Street, in
      <a href="/wiki/Braintree,_Massachusetts" title="Braintree, Massachusetts">
       Braintree, Massachusetts
      </a>
      , during the afternoon of April 15, 1920, robbers approached two men as they were transporting the company payroll into two large steel boxes to the main factory. Alessandro Berardelli, a security guard, was shot as he reached for his hip-holstered .38-caliber,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H_%26_R_Firearms" title="H &amp; R Firearms">
       Harrington &amp; Richardson
      </a>
      revolver, not recovered from the scene. Berardelli had been shot four times.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Frederick Parmenter, a paymaster who was unarmed, was shot twice:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      once in the chest and a second time, fatally, in the back as he attempted to flee.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The robbers seized the payroll boxes and escaped by climbing into a waiting getaway car, a stolen dark blue Buick. It sped away while the robbers fired wildly at company workers nearby.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="598" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg/220px-Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg/330px-Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg/440px-Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Svage_Model_1907_French.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Savage Model 1907 semi-automatic pistol
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="648" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg/220px-Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg/330px-Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg/440px-Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colt_Model_1908_Pocket_Hamerless_AdamsGuns_1783.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Colt Model 1903 Pocket Hammerless
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A coroner's report and subsequent ballistic investigation revealed that six bullets removed from the murdered men's bodies were of
      <a href="/wiki/.32_ACP" title=".32 ACP">
       .32 automatic (ACP) caliber
      </a>
      . Five of these .32-caliber bullets were all fired from a single automatic pistol, a .32-caliber
      <a href="/wiki/Savage_Model_1907" title="Savage Model 1907">
       Savage Model 1907
      </a>
      , which used a particularly narrow-grooved barrel rifling with a right-hand twist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two of the bullets were recovered from Berardelli's body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AND_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AND-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Four .32 automatic brass shell casings were found at the murder scene, each manufactured by one of three firms: Peters, Winchester, and Remington. The Winchester cartridge case was of a relatively obsolescent cartridge loading, which had been discontinued from production some years earlier.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two days after the robbery, police located the robbers' stolen getaway Buick; several 12-gauge shotgun shells were found on the ground nearby.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Arrests_and_indictment">
       Arrests and indictment
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Arrests and indictment">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Police suspicions regarding the Braintree robbery-murder and an earlier attempted robbery of another shoe factory on December 24, 1919 in
      <a href="/wiki/Bridgewater,_Massachusetts" title="Bridgewater, Massachusetts">
       Bridgewater
      </a>
      , centered on local Italian anarchists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVR1_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AVR1-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While neither Sacco nor Vanzetti had a criminal record, the authorities knew them as radical
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrorist" title="Terrorist">
       militants
      </a>
      and adherents of Galleani. Police speculated that the anarchists perpetrated the robberies to finance additional bombings like the
      <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_bombing" title="Wall Street bombing">
       Wall Street bombing
      </a>
      of September 1920.
     </p>
     <p>
      On April 16, one day after the robbery-murders, the
      <a href="/wiki/Immigration_and_Naturalization_Service" title="Immigration and Naturalization Service">
       Federal Immigration Service
      </a>
      called local police chief Michael E. Stewart to discuss Galleanist and anarchist Ferruccio Coacci, whom he had arrested on their behalf two years earlier. Coacci had succeeded in postponing his deportation until April 15, 1920, the day of the Braintree holdup. The FIS asked Stewart to investigate Coacci's excuse that he had failed to report for deportation on April 15 because his wife had fallen ill. Stewart sent two policemen to Coacci's house on April 11.
     </p>
     <p>
      When asked if Coacci owned a gun,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Housemate" title="Housemate">
       housemate
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Mario_Buda" title="Mario Buda">
       Mario Buda
      </a>
      stated that Coacci owned a .32 Savage automatic pistol, which he kept in the kitchen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A search of the kitchen revealed no gun, but Chief Stewart found a manufacturer's technical diagram for a Model 1907 .32 Savage automatic pistol  the exact pistol type and caliber used to shoot Parmenter and Berardelli  in a kitchen drawer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stewart asked Boda if he owned a gun, and the man produced a .32-caliber Spanish-made automatic pistol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_p._202_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_p._202-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Boda told police that he owned a 1914 Overland automobile, which was being repaired.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202_27-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From tracks found near the abandoned Buick getaway car, Chief Stewart surmised that two cars had been used in the getaway, and that Boda's car might have been the second car.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202_27-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When Stewart discovered that Coacci had worked for both the plants that had been robbed, he returned with the Bridgewater police. But 'Mike' Boda (aka Mario Buda) had since disappeared, along with his possessions and furniture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202_27-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_pp._201-202-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The police had instructed the Johnson garage, where the impounded cars were held, to notify them when the owners came to collect the 1914 Overland. On May 5, 1920, Mario Buda arrived at the garage with three other men, later identified as Sacco, Vanzetti, and Riccardo Orciani. The four men knew each other well; Buda would later call Sacco and Vanzetti "the best friends I had in America."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Police were alerted, but the men sensed a trap and fled. Buda escaped on a motorcycle with Orciani and did not resurface until 1928 in Italy.
     </p>
     <p>
      Sacco and Vanzetti jumped onto a streetcar, but were tracked down and soon arrested. When searched by police, both denied owning any guns, but were found to be holding loaded pistols. Sacco was found to have an Italian passport, anarchist literature, a loaded .32 Colt Model 1903 automatic pistol, and twenty-three .32 Automatic cartridges in his possession; several of these cases were of the same obsolescent type as the empty Winchester .32 casing found at the crime scene, and others were manufactured by the firms of Peters and Remington, much like other casings found at the scene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vanzetti had four 12-gauge shotgun shells
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_p._202_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Avrich.2C_Paul_1991_p._202-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a five-shot nickel-plated .38-caliber Harrington &amp; Richardson revolver identical to that carried by Berardelli, the slain Braintree guard, which had gone missing after the robbery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Under questioning, the pair denied any connection to anarchists.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following Sacco and Vanzetti's indictment for murder for the Braintree robbery, Galleanists and anarchists in the United States and abroad began a campaign of violent retaliation. On September 16, 1920, two days later, Mario Buda allegedly orchestrated the
      <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_bombing" title="Wall Street bombing">
       Wall Street bombing
      </a>
      , where a time-delay dynamite bomb packed with heavy iron sash-weights in a horse-drawn cart exploded, killing 38 people and wounding 134.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVR1_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AVR1-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1921, a booby trap bomb mailed to the American
      <a href="/wiki/Ambassador" title="Ambassador">
       ambassador
      </a>
      in Paris exploded, wounding his valet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the next six years, bombs exploded at other American embassies all over the world .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Trials">
       Trials
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Trials">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bridgewater_trial">
       Bridgewater trial
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bridgewater trial">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Only Vanzetti was tried for the attempted robbery and attempted murder in Bridgewater, which occurred on
      <a href="/wiki/Christmas_Eve" title="Christmas Eve">
       Christmas Eve
      </a>
      , December 24, 1919. Others arrested supported their alibis with documentation, like Sacco's time-card which suggests he was on the clock when the abortive Bridgewater robbery was committed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1927, advocates for Sacco and Vanzetti charged that this case was brought first as a conviction for the Bridgewater crimes would help convict him for the Braintree crimes, where evidence against him was weak. The prosecution countered that the timing was driven by the schedules of different courts that handled the cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On the recommendation of supporters, Vanzetti chose to be represented by John P. Vahey, an experienced defense attorney, rather than accept counsel appointed by the court.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Frederick Katzmann, Norfolk and Plymouth County District Attorney, prosecuted the case.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The presiding judge was
      <a href="/wiki/Webster_Thayer" title="Webster Thayer">
       Webster Thayer
      </a>
      , who was already assigned to the court before this case was scheduled. A few weeks earlier he had given a speech to new American citizens decrying Bolshevism and anarchism's threat to American institutions. He supported the suppression of functionally violent radical speech, and incitement to commit violent acts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The trial began on June 22, 1920. The prosecution presented several witnesses who put Vanzetti at the scene of the attempted robbery. Their descriptions varied, especially with respect to the shape and length of Vanzetti's mustache.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Physical evidence included a shotgun shell retrieved at the scene of the crime and several shells found on Vanzetti when he was arrested.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The defense produced sixteen witnesses, all Italians from Plymouth, who testified that at the time of the attempted robbery they had bought
      <a href="/wiki/Eel_as_food" title="Eel as food">
       eels
      </a>
      for the Christmas holiday from Vanzetti, in accordance with their
      <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">
       Christmas
      </a>
      traditions. Such details reinforced the difference between the Italians and the jurors. Some testified in imperfect English, others through an interpreter, whose failure to speak the same dialect of Italian as the witnesses hampered his effectiveness. On cross examination, the prosecution found it easy to make the witnesses appear confused about dates. A boy who testified admitted to rehearsing his testimony. "You learned it just like a piece at school?" the prosecutor asked. "Sure," he replied.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The defense tried to rebut the eyewitnesses with testimony that Vanzetti always wore his mustache in a distinctive long style, but the prosecution rebutted this.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Though the defense case went badly, Vanzetti did not testify in his own defense.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vanzetti, in 1927, said his lawyers opposed putting him on the stand.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      That same year, Vahey told the governor that Vanzetti had refused his advice to testify.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Decades later, a lawyer who assisted Vahey in the defense said that the defense attorneys left the choice to Vanzetti, but warned him that it would be difficult to prevent the prosecution from using cross examination to impeach his character based on his political beliefs. He said that Vanzetti chose not to testify after consulting with Sacco.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Herbert Ehrmann, who later joined the defense team, wrote many years later that the dangers of putting Vanzetti on the stand were very real.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrmann.2C_114-5_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ehrmann.2C_114-5-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another legal analysis of the case concluded that the defense would have little to lose from Vanzetti's testimony, since his conviction looked certain given how poorly his alibi witnesses had performed under cross-examination. That analysis deemed the defense overall "unconvincing" and "not closely argued or vigorously fought."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Vanzetti complained during his sentencing on April 9, 1927, for the Braintree crimes that Vahey "sold me for thirty golden money like Judas sold Jesus Christ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He accused Vahey of having conspired with the prosecutor "to agitate still more the passion of the juror, the prejudice of the juror" towards "people of our principles, against the foreigner, against slackers."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On July 1, 1927, the jury deliberated for five hours and returned guilty verdicts on both counts, attempted robbery and attempted murder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before sentencing, Thayer learned that during deliberations, the jury had tampered with the shells found on Vanzetti at the time of his arrest to determine if the shot they contained was of sufficient size to kill a man.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joughin.2C_56_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Joughin.2C_56-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since that prejudiced the jury's verdict on the attempted murder charge, Thayer ignored that conviction. On August 16, 1920, he sentenced Vanzetti for attempted robbery to a term of 12 to 15 years in prison, the maximum sentence allowed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WA_34-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-WA-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrmann.2C_114-5_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ehrmann.2C_114-5-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joughin.2C_56_50-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Joughin.2C_56-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An assessment of Thayer's conduct of the trial said "his stupid rulings as to the admissibility of conversations are about equally divided" between the two sides and thus provided no evidence of partiality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The defense raised only minor objections in an appeal that was not accepted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A few years later, Vahey joined Katzmanns law firm.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dedham_trial">
       Dedham trial
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Dedham trial">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NorfolkCoCourt.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2366" data-file-width="2658" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/NorfolkCoCourt.JPG/220px-NorfolkCoCourt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/NorfolkCoCourt.JPG/330px-NorfolkCoCourt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/NorfolkCoCourt.JPG/440px-NorfolkCoCourt.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NorfolkCoCourt.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_County_Courthouse" title="Norfolk County Courthouse">
         Norfolk County Courthouse
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dedham,_MA" title="Dedham, MA">
         Dedham, MA
        </a>
        , site of the second trial
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Sacco and Vanzetti both stood trial in
      <a href="/wiki/Dedham,_Massachusetts" title="Dedham, Massachusetts">
       Dedham, Massachusetts
      </a>
      , for the South Braintree robbery and murders, with
      <a href="/wiki/Webster_Thayer" title="Webster Thayer">
       Webster Thayer
      </a>
      again presiding; he had asked to be assigned the trial. Anticipating a possible bomb attack, authorities had the Dedham courtroom outfitted with cast-iron shutters, painted to appear wooden, and heavy, sliding steel doors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LIN_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LIN-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAT_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WAT-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Each day during the trial, the courthouse was placed under heavy police security, and Sacco and Vanzetti were escorted in and out of the courtroom by armed guards.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LIN_54-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-LIN-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WAT_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-WAT-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Commonwealth relied on evidence that Sacco was absent from his work, in a shoe factory, on the day of the murder; that the defendants were in the neighborhood of the scene of the Braintree robber-murder scene on the morning when it occurred, being identified as having been there seen separately and also together; that the Buick getaway car was also in the neighborhood and that Vanzetti was near and in it; that Sacco was seen near the scene of the murder before it occurred and also was seen to shoot Berardelli after he fell and that that shot caused his death; that used shell casings were left at the scene of the murder, some of which could have been found to have been discharged from a .32 pistol afterwards found on Sacco; that a cap was found at the scene of the murder, which witnesses identified as a cap that resembled one formerly worn by Sacco; and, that both men were members of anarchist cells that espoused violence, including assassination.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Among the more important witnesses called by the prosecution was salesman Carlos E. Goodridge, who stated that as the getaway car raced within twenty-five feet of him, one of the car's occupants pointed a gun in his direction, whom he identified as being Sacco.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Both men offered alibis backed by several witnesses. Vanzetti testified that he had been selling fish at the time of the Braintree robbery. Sacco testified that he had been in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" title="Boston, Massachusetts">
       Boston
      </a>
      applying for a passport at the Italian
      <a href="/wiki/Consul_(representative)" title="Consul (representative)">
       consulate
      </a>
      . He stated he had lunched in Boston's North end with several friends, each of whom testified on his behalf. Prior to the trial, Sacco's lawyer,
      <a href="/wiki/Fred_Moore_(attorney)" title="Fred Moore (attorney)">
       Fred Moore
      </a>
      , went to great lengths to contact the consulate employee whom Sacco said he had talked with on the afternoon of the crime. Once contacted in Italy, the clerk said he remembered Sacco because of the unusually large passport photo he presented. The clerk also remembered the date, April 15, 1920, but he refused to return to the United States to testify (a trip requiring two ship voyages), citing his ill health. Instead he executed a sworn deposition that was read aloud in court and questioned by the prosecution, which argued Sacco's visit to the consulate could not be established with certainty. The prosecution presented witnesses who said that Sacco's lunch companions were fellow
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">
       anarchists
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Much of the trial focused on material evidence, notably bullets, guns, and a cap. Prosecution witnesses testified that
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      , the .32-caliber bullet that had fatally wounded Berardelli, was from a discontinued Winchester .32 Auto cartridge loading so obsolete that the only bullets similar to it that anyone could locate to make comparisons were those found in the cartridges in Sacco's pockets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-truTV_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-truTV-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prosecutor Frederick Katzmann decided to participate in a forensic bullet examination using bullets test-fired from Sacco's .32 Colt Automatic after the defense arranged for such tests. Sacco, saying he had nothing to hide, had allowed his gun to be test-fired, with experts for both sides present, during the trial's second week. The prosecution matched bullets fired through the gun to those taken from one of the slain guards.
     </p>
     <p>
      In court, District Attorney Katzmann called two forensic gun expert witnesses, Capt. Charles Van Amburgh of Springfield Armory and Capt. William Proctor of the Massachusetts State Police, who testified that they believed that of the four bullets recovered from Berardelli's body,
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
       the fatal bullet  exhibited rifling marks consistent with those found on bullets fired from Sacco's .32 Colt Automatic pistol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AND_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AND-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Capt. Proctor would later sign an affidavit stating that he could not positively identify Sacco's .32 Colt as the only pistol that could have fired
      <i>
       Bullet III.
      </i>
      This meant that
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      could have been fired from any of the 300,000 .32 Colt Automatic pistols then in circulation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRA_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GRA-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEV_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEV-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In rebuttal, two defense forensic gun experts would later testify that Bullet III did not match any of the test bullets from Sacco's Colt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Noting all the witnesses to the shooting testified that they saw one gunman shoot Berardelli four times, the defense questioned how only one of four bullets found in the deceased guard was identified as being fired from Sacco's Colt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AND_24-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-AND-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Vanzetti was being tried under Massachusetts' felony-murder rule, and the prosecution sought to implicate him in the South Braintree robbery by the testimony of several witnesses: one testified that he was in the getaway car, and others who stated they saw Vanzetti in the vicinity of the South Braintree factory around the time of the robbery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      No direct evidence tied Vanzetti's .38 nickel-plated Harrington &amp; Richardson five-shot revolver to the crime scene, except for the fact that it was identical in type and appearance to the slain guard Berardelli's, which had been missing since after the shooting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was undisputed that all six of the bullets recovered from both the murder victims were of .32 Automatic caliber and had been fired from at least two different
      <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_pistol" title="Semi-automatic pistol">
       automatic pistols
      </a>
      . But, Vanzetti carried a .38-caliber
      <a href="/wiki/Revolver" title="Revolver">
       revolver
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The prosecution claimed Vanzetti's .38 revolver had originally belonged to the slain Berardelli, and that it had been stolen during the robbery. No one testified to seeing anyone take the gun, but Berardelli had an empty holster and no gun on him when he was found.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, witnesses to the payroll shooting had described Berardelli as reaching for his gun on his hip when he was cut down by pistol fire by the robbers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      District Attorney Katzmann pointed out that Vanzetti had lied at the time of his arrest, when making statements about the .38 revolver found in his possession. He claimed that the revolver was his own, and that he carried it for self-protection, yet he incorrectly described it to police as a six-shot revolver instead of a five-shot.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vanzetti also told police that he had purchased only one box of cartridges for the gun, all of the same make, yet his revolver was loaded with five .38 cartridges of varying brands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the time of his arrest, Vanzetti also claimed that he had bought the gun at a store (but could not remember which one), and that it cost $18 or $19 (three times its actual market value).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bortman.2C_Eli_2005_p._40_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bortman.2C_Eli_2005_p._40-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He lied about where he had obtained the .38 cartridges found in the revolver.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In an attempt to show that Vanzetti's revolver was taken from the slain Berardelli, the prosecution traced the history of Berardelli's .38 Harrington &amp; Richardson (H&amp;R) revolver. Berardelli's wife testified that she and her husband dropped off the gun for repair at the Iver Johnson Co. of Boston a few weeks before the murder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the foreman of the Iver Johnson repair shop, Berardelli's revolver was given a repair tag with the number of 94765, and this number was recorded in the repair logbook with the statement "H. &amp; R. revolver, .38-calibre, new hammer, repairing, half an hour".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the shop books did not record the gun's serial number, and the caliber was apparently incorrectly labeled as .32 instead of .38-caliber.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The shop foreman testified that a new spring and hammer were put into Berardelli's Harrington &amp; Richardson revolver. It was claimed and the half-hour repair paid for, though the date and identity of the claimant was not recorded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After examining Vanzetti's .38 revolver, the foreman testified that Vanzetti's gun had a new replacement hammer in keeping with the repair performed on Berardelli's revolver.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson.2C_Bruce_p._126_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Watson.2C_Bruce_p._126-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The foreman explained that the shop was always kept busy repairing 20 to 30 revolvers per day, which made it very hard to remember individual guns or keep reliable records of when they were picked up by their owners.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869_22-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-Reed.2C_Barry_C._1960.2C_pp._867-869-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      But, he said that unclaimed guns were sold by Iver Johnson at the end of each year, and the shop had no record of an unclaimed gun sale of Berardelli's revolver.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson.2C_Bruce_p._126_65-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Watson.2C_Bruce_p._126-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      To reinforce the conclusion that Berardelli had reclaimed his revolver from the repair shop, the prosecution called a witness who testified that he had seen Berardelli in possession of a .38 nickel-plated revolver of the same type found on Vanzetti the Saturday night before the Braintree robbery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The defense, noting that the repair shop had no record of the gun ever being picked up by Berardelli, put on testimony by Vanzetti that  in contradiction to what he had told police upon his arrest  he had bought the .38 revolver secondhand from a friend for five dollars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bortman.2C_Eli_2005_p._40_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bortman.2C_Eli_2005_p._40-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This was corroborated by Luigi Falzini (Falsini), a friend of Vanzetti's and a fellow Galleanist, who stated that, after buying the .38 revolver from one Riccardo Orciani,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      he sold it to Vanzetti.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bortman.2C_Eli_2005_p._40_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Bortman.2C_Eli_2005_p._40-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The defense also called two expert witnesses, a Mr. Burns and a Mr. Fitzgerald, who each testified that no new spring and hammer had ever been installed in the revolver found in Vanzetti's possession.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The District Attorney's final piece of material evidence was a flop-eared cap claimed to have been Sacco's. Sacco tried the cap on in court and, according to two newspaper sketch artists who ran cartoons the next day, it was too small, sitting high on his head. But Katzmann insisted the cap fitted Sacco and continued to refer to it as his.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="1039" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg/220px-Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg/330px-Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg/440px-Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Save_Sacco_and_Vanzetti.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Protest for Sacco and Vanzetti in
        <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">
         London
        </a>
        , 1921
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Controversy clouded the prosecution witnesses who identified Sacco at the scene of the crime. One, a bookkeeper named Mary Splaine, precisely described Sacco as the man she saw firing from the getaway car. Yet cross examination revealed that Splaine had refused to identify Sacco at the inquest and had seen the getaway car for only a second and from nearly a half-block away. While a few others singled out Sacco or Vanzetti as the men they had seen at the scene of the crime, far more witnesses, both prosecution and defense, refused to identify them.
     </p>
     <p>
      The defendants' radical politics may have played a role in the verdict. Thayer, though a sworn enemy of anarchists, warned the defense against bringing anarchism into the trial. Yet defense attorney Fred Moore felt he had to call both Sacco and Vanzetti as witnesses to let them explain why they were fully armed when arrested. Both men testified that they had been rounding up radical literature when apprehended, and that they had feared another government deportation raid. Yet both hurt their case with rambling discourses on radical politics that the prosecution mocked. The prosecution also brought out that both men had fled the draft by going to Mexico in 1917.
     </p>
     <p>
      After deliberating for three hours, then breaking for dinner, the jury returned guilty verdicts. Supporters later insisted Sacco and Vanzetti had been convicted for their anarchist views, yet every juror insisted anarchism had played no part in their decision. First-degree murder in Massachusetts was a
      <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">
       capital
      </a>
      crime. Sacco and Vanzetti were bound for the electric chair unless the defense could find new evidence.
     </p>
     <p>
      The verdicts and the likelihood of death sentences immediately roused international opinion. There were demonstrations in 60 Italian cities and a flood of mail to the American embassy in Paris. Demonstrations followed in a number of Latin American cities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Anatole_France" title="Anatole France">
       Anatole France
      </a>
      , veteran of the campaign for
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Dreyfus" title="Alfred Dreyfus">
       Alfred Dreyfus
      </a>
      and recipient of the 1921
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Literature" title="Nobel Prize for Literature">
       Nobel Prize for Literature
      </a>
      , wrote an "Appeal to the American People": "The death of Sacco and Vanzetti will make martyrs of them and cover you with shame. You are a great people. You ought to be a just people."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Defense_Committee">
       Defense Committee
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Defense Committee">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The Sacco-Vanzetti Defense Committee was formed on May 9, 1920, immediately following the arrests, by a group of fellow anarchists, headed by Vanzetti's 23-year-old friend Aldino Felicani. Over the next seven years, it raised $300,000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Fred Moore drew on its funds for his investigations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Differences arose when Moore tried to determine who had committed the South Braintree crimes over objections from anarchists that he was doing the government's work. After the Committee hired William G. Thomson to manage the legal defense, he objected to its propaganda efforts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A Defense Committee publicist wrote an article about the first trial that was published in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">
        The New Republic
       </a>
      </i>
      . In the winter of 1920-21, the Defense Committee sent stories to labor union publications every week. It produced pamphlets with titles like
      <i>
       Fangs at Labor's Throat
      </i>
      , sometimes printing thousands of copies. It sent speakers to Italian communities in factory towns and mining camps.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Committee eventually added staff from outside the anarchist movement, notably Mary Donovan, a 40-year-old who had experience as a labor leader and
      <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn Fin">
       Sinn Fin
      </a>
      organizer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1927, she and Felicani together recruited Gardner Jackson, a
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston_Globe" title="Boston Globe">
        Boston Globe
       </a>
      </i>
      reporter from a wealthy family, to manage publicity and serve as a mediator between the Committee's anarchists and the growing number of supporters with more liberal political views, who included socialites, lawyers, and intellectuals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Jackson bridged the gap between the radicals and the social elite so well that Sacco thanked him a few weeks before his execution:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       We are one heart, but unfortunately we represent two different class....But, whenever the heart of one of the upper class join with the exploited workers for the struggle of the right in the human feeling is the feel of an spontaneous attraction and brotherly love to one another.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         76
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The noted American author
      <a href="/wiki/John_Dos_Passos" title="John Dos Passos">
       John Dos Passos
      </a>
      joined the committee and wrote its 127-page official review of the case:
      <i>
       Facing the Chair: Story of Americanization of Two Foreignborn Workmen
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the executions, the Committee continued its work, helping to gather material that eventually appeared as
      <i>
       The Letters of Sacco and Vanzetti
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Motions_for_a_new_trial">
       Motions for a new trial
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Motions for a new trial">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Judge Thayer held hearings on five separate motions for a new trial for Sacco and Vanzetti in October and November 1923. Defense attorney
      <a href="/wiki/Fred_Moore_(attorney)" title="Fred Moore (attorney)">
       Fred Moore
      </a>
      presented affidavits and testimony by three prosecution witnesses, to the effect that they had been coerced into identifying Sacco at the scene of the crime. However, when confronted by District Attorney Katzmann, the three witnesses denied being subjected to any prosecution coercion. Lola Andrews told authorities that she was forced to sign an affidavit stating she had wrongfully identified Sacco and Vanzetti, but signed a counter-affidavit the following day. Another, Lewis Pelser, described how he had submitted to alleged prosecutorial coercion while drunk and signed a counter-affidavit shortly thereafter.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One motion, the so-called Hamilton-Proctor motion, involved the forensic ballistic evidence presented by the expert witnesses for the prosecution and defense. The prosecution's firearms expert, Charles Van Amburgh, had re-examined the evidence in preparation for the motion. By 1923, bullet comparison technology had improved somewhat, and Van Amburgh submitted photos of the bullets fired from Sacco's .32 Colt in support of the argument that they matched the bullet that killed Berardelli. In response, the controversial
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EVA_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-EVA-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      self-proclaimed "firearms expert" for the defense, Albert H. Hamilton,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EVA_79-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-EVA-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      conducted an in-court demonstration involving two brand new Colt .32-caliber automatic pistols belonging to Hamilton, along with Sacco's .32 Colt of the same make and caliber. In front of Judge Thayer and the lawyers for both sides, Hamilton disassembled all three pistols and placed the major component parts  barrel, barrel bushing, recoil spring, frame, slide, and magazine  into three piles on the table before him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JOU_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JOU-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FISH_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FISH-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
