       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He explained the functions of each part and began to demonstrate how each was interchangeable, in the process intermingling the parts of all three pistols.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FISH_82-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FISH-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Judge Thayer stopped Hamilton and demanded that he reassemble Sacco's pistol with its proper parts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FISH_82-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-FISH-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other motions focused on the jury foreman and a prosecution ballistics expert. In 1923, the defense filed an affidavit from a friend of the jury foreman, who swore that prior to the trial, the jury foreman had allegedly said of Sacco and Vanzetti, "Damn them, they ought to hang them anyway!" That same year, the defense read to the court an affidavit by Captain William Proctor (who had died shortly after conclusion of the trial) in which Proctor stated that he could not say that
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      was fired by Sacco's .32 Colt pistol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEV_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEV-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the conclusion of the appeal hearings, Thayer denied all motions for a new trial on October 1, 1924.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Several months later, in February 1924, Judge Thayer asked one of the firearms experts for the prosecution, Capt. Charles Van Amburgh, to reinspect Sacco's Colt and determine its condition. With District Attorney Katzmann present, Van Amburgh took the gun from the clerk and started to take it apart.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FISH_82-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-FISH-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Van Amburgh quickly noticed that the barrel to Sacco's gun was brand new, being still covered in the manufacturer's protective
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmoline" title="Cosmoline">
       rust preventative
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FISH_82-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-FISH-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Judge Thayer began private hearings to determine who had tampered with the evidence by switching the barrel on Sacco's gun. During three weeks of hearings, Albert Hamilton and Captain Van Amburgh squared off, challenging each other's authority. Testimony suggested that Sacco's gun had been treated with little care, and frequently disassembled for inspection. New defense attorney William Thompson insisted that no one on his side could have switched the barrels "unless they wanted to run their necks into a noose."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Albert Hamilton swore he had only taken the gun apart while being watched by Judge Thayer. Judge Thayer made no finding as to who had switched the .32 Colt barrels, but ordered the rusty barrel returned to Sacco's Colt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FISH_82-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-FISH-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the hearing concluded, unannounced to Judge Thayer, Captain Van Amburgh took both Sacco's and Vanzetti's guns, along with the bullets and shells involved in the crime to his home where he kept them until a Boston Globe expose revealed the misappropriation in 1960. Meanwhile, Van Amburgh bolstered his own credentials by writing an article on the case for True Detective Mysteries. The 1935 article charged that prior to the discovery of the gun barrel switch, Albert Hamilton had tried to walk out of the courtroom with Sacco's gun but was stopped by Judge Thayer. Although several historians of the case, including Francis Russell, have reported this story as factual, nowhere in transcripts of the private hearing on the gun barrel switch was this incident ever mentioned. The same year the True Detective article was published, a study of ballistics in the case concluded, "what might have been almost indubitable evidence was in fact rendered more than useless by the bungling of the experts."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Appeal_to_the_Supreme_Judicial_Court">
       Appeal to the Supreme Judicial Court
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Appeal to the Supreme Judicial Court">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The defense appealed Thayer's denial of their motions to the
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Supreme_Judicial_Court" title="Massachusetts Supreme Judicial Court">
       Supreme Judicial Court
      </a>
      (SJC), the highest level of the state's judicial system. Both sides presented arguments to its five judges on January 1113, 1926.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The SJC returned a unanimous verdict on May 12, 1926 upholding Judge Thayer's decisions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Court did not have the authority to review the trial record as a whole or to judge the fairness of the case. Instead, the judges considered only whether Thayer had abused his discretion in the course of the trial. Thayer later claimed that the SJC had "approved" the verdicts, which advocates for the defendants protested as a misinterpretation of the Court's ruling, which only found "no error" in his individual rulings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Madeiros_confession">
       Madeiros confession
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Madeiros confession">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In November 1925, Celestino Madeiros, an ex-convict awaiting trial for murder, confessed to committing the Braintree crimes. He absolved Sacco and Vanzetti of participation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In May, once the SJC had denied their appeal and Madeiros was convicted, the defense investigated the details of Madeiros' story. Police interviews led them to the Morelli gang based in Providence, Rhode Island. They developed an alternative theory of the crime based on the gangs history of shoe-factory robberies, connections to a car like that used in Braintree, and other details. Gang leader Joe Morelli bore a striking resemblance to Sacco.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The defense filed a motion for a new trial based on the Madeiros confession on May 26, 1926.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In support of their motion they included 64
      <a href="/wiki/Affidavit" title="Affidavit">
       affidavits
      </a>
      . The prosecution countered with 26 affidavits.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When Thayer heard arguments from September 13 to 17, 1926,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the defense, along with their Madeiros-Morelli theory of the crime, charged that the U.S. Justice Department was aiding the prosecution by withholding information obtained in its own investigation of the case. Attorney William Thompson made an explicitly political attack: "A government which has come to value its own secrets more than it does the lives of its citizens has become a tyranny, whether you call it a republic, a monarchy, or anything else!"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Judge Thayer denied this motion for a new trial on October 23, 1926. After arguing against the credibility of Madeiros, he addressed the defense claims against the federal government, saying the defense was suffering from "a new type of disease,...a belief in the existence of something which in fact and truth has no such existence."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Three days later, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Boston_Herald" title="Boston Herald">
        Boston Herald
       </a>
      </i>
      responded to Thayer's decision by reversing its longstanding position and calling for a new trial. Its editorial, "We Submit", earned its author a
      <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">
       Pulitzer Prize
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      No other newspapers followed suit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Second_appeal_to_the_Supreme_Judicial_Court">
       Second appeal to the Supreme Judicial Court
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Second appeal to the Supreme Judicial Court">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The defense promptly appealed again to the Supreme Judicial Court and presented their arguments on January 27 and 28, 1927.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the appeal was under consideration, Harvard law professor and future
      <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">
       Supreme Court
      </a>
      Justice
      <a href="/wiki/Felix_Frankfurter" title="Felix Frankfurter">
       Felix Frankfurter
      </a>
      published an article in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Atlantic" title="The Atlantic">
        Atlantic Monthly
       </a>
      </i>
      arguing for a retrial. He noted that the SJC had already taken a very narrow view of its authority when considering the first appeal, and called upon the court to review the entire record of the case. He called their attention to Thayer's lengthy statement that accompanied his denial of the Madeiros appeal, describing it as "a farrago of misquotations, misrepresentations, suppressions, and mutilations," "honeycombed with demonstrable errors."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the same time, Major
      <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Goddard_(ballistics)" title="Calvin Goddard (ballistics)">
       Calvin Goddard
      </a>
      , a ballistics expert who had helped pioneer the use of the comparison microscope in forensic ballistic research, offered to conduct an independent examination of the forensic gun and bullet evidence using techniques he had developed for use with the comparison microscope.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOD_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOD-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Goddard first offered to conduct a new forensic examination for the defense, which rejected it, and then to the prosecution, which accepted his offer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOD_100-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOD-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Using the comparison microscope, Goddard compared
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      and a .32 Auto shell casing found at the South Braintree shooting with that of several .32 Auto test cartridges fired from Sacco's .32 Colt automatic pistol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRA_59-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GRA-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOD_100-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOD-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Goddard concluded that not only did
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      match the rifling marks found on the barrel of Sacco's .32 Colt pistol, but that scratches made by the firing pin of Sacco's .32 Colt on the primers of spent shell casings test-fired from Sacco's Colt matched those found on the primer of a spent shell casing recovered at the South Braintree murder scene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRA_59-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-GRA-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOD_100-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOD-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      More sophisticated comparative examinations in 1935, 1961, and 1983 each reconfirmed the opinion that the bullet the prosecution said killed Berardelli, and one of the cartridge cases introduced into evidence, were fired in Sacco's .32 Colt automatic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRA_59-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-GRA-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (Defenders of Sacco and Vanzetti have long claimed that "Bullet III" and the associated cartridge case were planted by the police, in order to frame the two defendants. William Young and David Kaiser, in their book "Postmortem: New Evidence in the Sacco-Vanzetti Case," note that the scratches at the base of Bullet III are made in a handwriting that is dramatically different than those at the base of the other bullets.)
     </p>
     <p>
      The Supreme Judicial Court denied the Madeiros appeal on April 5, 1927.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmannxvii_84-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmannxvii-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Summarizing the decision, the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">
        New York Times
       </a>
      </i>
      said that the SJC had determined that "the judge had a right to rule as he did" but that the SJC "did not deny the validity of the new evidence."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytseven_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytseven-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The SJC also said: "It is not imperative that a new trial be granted even though evidence is newly discovered and, if presented to a jury, would justify a different verdict."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Protests_and_advocacy">
       Protests and advocacy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Protests and advocacy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Many famous
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">
       socialists
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Intellectual" title="Intellectual">
       intellectuals
      </a>
      campaigned for a retrial without success.
      <a href="/wiki/John_Dos_Passos" title="John Dos Passos">
       John Dos Passos
      </a>
      came to Boston to cover the case as a journalist, stayed to author a pamphlet called
      <i>
       Facing the Chair
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and was arrested in a demonstration on August 10, 1927, along with
      <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Parker" title="Dorothy Parker">
       Dorothy Parker
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After being arrested while picketing the State House, the poet
      <a href="/wiki/Edna_St._Vincent_Millay" title="Edna St. Vincent Millay">
       Edna St. Vincent Millay
      </a>
      pleaded her case to the governor in person and then wrote an appeal: "I cry to you with a million voices: answer our doubt...There is need in Massachusetts of a great man tonight."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Others who wrote to Fuller or signed petitions included
      <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">
       Albert Einstein
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">
       George Bernard Shaw
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">
       H. G. Wells
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The president of the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_Labor" title="American Federation of Labor">
       American Federation of Labor
      </a>
      cited "the long period of time intervening between the commission of the crime and the final decision of the Court" as well as "the mental and physical anguish which Sacco and Vanzetti must have undergone during the past seven years" in a telegram to the governor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">
       Benito Mussolini
      </a>
      , the target of two anarchist assassination attempts, quietly made inquiries through diplomatic channels and was prepared to ask Governor Fuller to commute the sentences if it appeared his request would be granted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1926, a bomb presumed to be the work of anarchists destroyed the house of Samuel Johnson, the brother of the Simon Johnson who had called police the night of Sacco and Vanzetti's arrest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In August 1927, the
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Workers_of_the_World" title="Industrial Workers of the World">
       Industrial Workers of the World
      </a>
      (IWW) called for a three-day nationwide walkout to protest the pending executions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The most notable response came in the
      <a href="/wiki/Walsenburg,_Colorado" title="Walsenburg, Colorado">
       Walsenburg
      </a>
      coal district of Colorado, where 1,132 out of 1,167 miners participated in the walkout. It led to the
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Workers_of_the_World_philosophy_and_tactics#Colorado_coal_strike_.28a_case_study.29" title="Industrial Workers of the World philosophy and tactics">
       Colorado coal strike of 1927
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Defendants_in_prison">
       Defendants in prison
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Defendants in prison">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:State_prison_1900.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="400" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/State_prison_1900.jpg/220px-State_prison_1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/State_prison_1900.jpg/330px-State_prison_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/State_prison_1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:State_prison_1900.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Charlestown_State_Prison" title="Charlestown State Prison">
         Charlestown State Prison
        </a>
        , 1900
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      For their part, Sacco and Vanzetti seemed to alternate between moods of defiance, vengeance, resignation, and despair. The June 1926 issue of
      <i>
       Protesta Umana,
      </i>
      published by their Defense Committee, carried an article signed by Sacco and Vanzetti that appealed for retaliation by their colleagues. In the article, Vanzetti wrote, "I will try to see Thayer death  [
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">
        sic
       </a>
      </i>
      ] before his pronunciation of our sentence," and asked fellow anarchists for "revenge, revenge in our names and the names of our living and dead."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The article concluded by urging readers to recall
      <i>
       La Salute  in voi!
      </i>
      , Galleani's bomb-making manual.
     </p>
     <p>
      Both wrote dozens of letters asserting their innocence, insisting they had been framed because they were anarchists. Their conduct in prison consistently impressed guards and wardens. In 1927, the Dedham jail chaplain wrote to the head of an investigatory commission that he had seen no evidence of guilt or remorse on Sacco's part. Vanzetti impressed fellow prisoners at
      <a href="/wiki/Charlestown_State_Prison" title="Charlestown State Prison">
       Charlestown State Prison
      </a>
      as a bookish intellectual, incapable of committing any violent crime. Novelist John Dos Passos, who visited both men in jail, observed of Vanzetti, "nobody in his right mind who was planning such a crime would take a man like that along."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vanzetti developed his command of English to such a degree that journalist
      <a href="/wiki/Murray_Kempton" title="Murray Kempton">
       Murray Kempton
      </a>
      later described him as "the greatest writer of English in our century to learn his craft, do his work, and die all in the space of seven years."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sentencing">
       Sentencing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sentencing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      On April 9, 1927, Judge Thayer heard final statements from Sacco and Vanzetti. In a lengthy speech Vanzetti said:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmann458_116-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmann458-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       I would not wish to a dog or to a snake, to the most low and misfortunate creature of the earth, I would not wish to any of them what I have had to suffer for things that I am not guilty of. But my conviction is that I have suffered for things that I am guilty of. I am suffering because I am a radical and indeed I am a radical; I have suffered because I am an Italian and indeed I am an Italian...if you could execute me two times, and if I could be reborn two other times, I would live again to do what I have done already.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         118
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Thayer declared that the responsibility for the conviction rested solely with the jury's determination of guilt. "The Court has absolutely nothing to do with that question." He sentenced each of them to "suffer the punishment of death by the passage of a current of electricity through your body" during the week beginning July 10.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ehrmann458_116-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ehrmann458-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He twice postponed the execution date while the governor considered requests for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Executive_clemency#State_law" title="Executive clemency">
       clemency
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On May 10, a package bomb addressed to Governor Fuller was intercepted in the Boston post office.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Clemency_appeal_and_the_Governor.27s_Advisory_Committee">
       Clemency appeal and the Governor's Advisory Committee
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Clemency appeal and the Governor's Advisory Committee">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alvin_T_Fuller.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="323" data-file-width="214" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d2/Alvin_T_Fuller.png/170px-Alvin_T_Fuller.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d2/Alvin_T_Fuller.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d2/Alvin_T_Fuller.png 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alvin_T_Fuller.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Massachusetts Governor
        <a href="/wiki/Alvan_T._Fuller" title="Alvan T. Fuller">
         Alvan T. Fuller
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In response to public protests that greeted the sentencing, Massachusetts Governor
      <a href="/wiki/Alvan_T._Fuller" title="Alvan T. Fuller">
       Alvan T. Fuller
      </a>
      faced last-minute appeals to grant clemency to Sacco and Vanzetti. On June 1, 1927, he appointed an Advisory Committee of three: President
      <a href="/wiki/Abbott_Lawrence_Lowell" title="Abbott Lawrence Lowell">
       Abbott Lawrence Lowell
      </a>
      of Harvard, President
      <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Wesley_Stratton" title="Samuel Wesley Stratton">
       Samuel Wesley Stratton
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">
       MIT
      </a>
      , and Probate Judge
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Grant_(novelist)" title="Robert Grant (novelist)">
       Robert Grant
      </a>
      . They were tasked with reviewing the trial to determine whether it had been fair. Lowell's appointment was generally well received, for though he had controversy in his past, he had also at times demonstrated an independent streak. The defense attorneys considered resigning when they determined that the Committee was biased against the defendants, but some of the defendants' most prominent supporters, including Harvard Law Professor
      <a href="/wiki/Felix_Frankfurter" title="Felix Frankfurter">
       Felix Frankfurter
      </a>
      and Judge
      <a href="/wiki/Julian_Mack" title="Julian Mack">
       Julian W. Mack
      </a>
      of the U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals, persuaded them to stay because Lowell "was not entirely hopeless."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One of the defense attorneys, though ultimately very critical of the Committee's work, thought the Committee members were not really capable of the task the Governor set for them:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       No member of the Committee had the essential sophistication that comes with experience in the trial of criminal cases....The high positions in the community held by the members of the Committee obscured the fact that they were not really qualified to perform the difficult task assigned to them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrmann.2C_255-6.2C_375.2C_512.2C_525ff_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ehrmann.2C_255-6.2C_375.2C_512.2C_525ff-122">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         122
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      He also thought that the Committee, particularly Lowell, imagined it could use its fresh and more powerful analytical abilities to outperform the efforts of those who had worked on the case for years, even finding evidence of guilt that professional prosecutors had discarded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehrmann.2C_255-6.2C_375.2C_512.2C_525ff_122-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ehrmann.2C_255-6.2C_375.2C_512.2C_525ff-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Grant was another establishment figure, a probate court judge from 1893 to 1923 and an Overseer of
      <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">
       Harvard University
      </a>
      from 1896 to 1921, and the author of a dozen popular novels.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some criticized Grant's appointment to the Committee, with one defense lawyer saying he "had a black-tie class concept of life around him," but
      <a href="/wiki/Harold_Laski" title="Harold Laski">
       Harold Laski
      </a>
      in a conversation at the time found him "moderate." Others cited evidence of xenophobia in some of his novels, references to "riff-raff" and a variety of racial slurs. His biographer allows that he was "not a good choice," not a legal scholar, and handicapped by age. Stratton, the one member who was not a "
      <a href="/wiki/Boston_Brahmin" title="Boston Brahmin">
       Boston Brahmin
      </a>
      ," maintained the lowest public profile of the three and hardly spoke during its hearings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In their earlier appeals, the defense was limited to the trial record. The Governor's Committee, however, was not a judicial proceeding, so Judge Thayer's comments outside the courtroom could be used to demonstrate his bias. Once Thayer told reporters that "No long-haired anarchist from California can run this court!"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the affidavits of eyewitnesses, Thayer also lectured members of his clubs, calling Sacco and Vanzetti "Bolsheviki!" and saying he would "get them good and proper". During the Dedham trial's first week, Thayer said to reporters: "Did you ever see a case in which so many leaflets and circulars have been spread...saying people couldn't get a fair trial in Massachusetts? You wait till I give my charge to the jury, I'll show them!"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1924, Thayer confronted a Massachusetts lawyer at Dartmouth, his
      <i>
       alma mater
      </i>
      , and said: "Did you see what I did with those anarchistic bastards the other day. I guess that will hold them for a while.... Let them go to the Supreme Court now and see what they can get out of them."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Committee knew that, following the verdict,
      <i>
       Boston Globe
      </i>
      reporter Frank Sibley, who had covered the trial, wrote a protest to the Massachusetts attorney general condemning Thayer's blatant bias. Thayer's behavior both inside the courtroom and outside of it had become a public issue, with the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_World" title="New York World">
        New York World
       </a>
      </i>
      attacking Thayer as "an agitated little man looking for publicity and utterly impervious to the ethical standards one has the right to expect of a man presiding in a capital case."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On July 1213, 1927, following testimony by the defense firearms expert Albert H. Hamilton before the Committee, the Assistant District Attorney for Massachusetts, Dudley P. Ranney, took the opportunity to cross-examine Hamilton. He submitted affidavits questioning Hamilton's credentials as well as his performance during the New York trial of Charles Stielow, in which Hamilton's testimony linking rifling marks to a bullet used to kill the victim nearly sent an innocent man to the electric chair.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EVA_79-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-EVA-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Committee also heard from Braintree's police chief who told them he had found the cap on Pearl Street, allegedly dropped by Sacco during the crime, a full 24-hours after the getaway car had fled the scene. The chief doubted the cap belonged to Sacco and called the whole trial a contest "to see who could tell the biggest lies."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After two weeks of hearing witnesses and reviewing evidence, the Committee determined that the trial had been fair and a new trial was not warranted. They assessed the charges against Thayer as well. Their criticism, using words provided by Judge Grant,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      was direct: "He ought not to have talked about the case off the bench, and doing so was a grave breach of judicial decorum." But they also found some of the charges about his statements unbelievable or exaggerated, and they determined that anything he might have said had no impact on the trial. The panel's reading of the trial transcript convinced them that Thayer "tried to be scrupulously fair." The Committee also reported that the trial jurors were almost unanimous in praising Thayer's conduct of the trial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A defense attorney later noted ruefully that the release of the Committee's report "abruptly stilled the burgeoning doubts among the leaders of opinion in New England."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Supporters of the convicted men denounced the Committee. Harold Laski told Holmes that the Committee's work showed that Lowell's "loyalty to his class ... transcended his ideas of logic and justice."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Execution_and_funeral">
       Execution and funeral
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Execution and funeral">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      On August 15, 1927, with the executions scheduled for midnight on August 22, a bomb exploded at the home of one of the Dedham jurors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On Sunday August 21, more than 20,000 protesters assembled on
      <a href="/wiki/Boston_Common" title="Boston Common">
       Boston Common
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In their cells at
      <a href="/wiki/Charlestown_State_Prison" title="Charlestown State Prison">
       Charlestown State Prison
      </a>
      , both Sacco and Vanzetti refused a priest several times on their last day due to both of them being militant atheists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Their attorney William Thompson asked Vanzetti to make a statement opposing violent retaliation for his death and they discussed forgiving one's enemies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thompson also asked him to swear to his and Sacco's innocence one last time, and Vanzetti did. Celestino Madeiros, whose execution had been delayed in case his testimony was required at another trial of Sacco and Vanzetti, was executed first. Sacco was next and walked quietly to the
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_chair" title="Electric chair">
       electric chair
      </a>
      , then shouted "Farewell, mother."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytexecution_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytexecution-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vanzetti, in his final moments, shook hands with guards and thanked them for their kind treatment, read a statement proclaiming his innocence, and finally said, "I wish to forgive some people for what they are now doing to me."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytexecution_4-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytexecution-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      All three executions were carried out by
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_G._Elliott" title="Robert G. Elliott">
       Robert G. Elliott
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_electrician" title="State electrician">
       state electrician
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the executions, death masks were made of the men.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Violent demonstrations swept through many cities the next day, including Geneva, London, Paris, Amsterdam, and Tokyo. In South America wildcat strikes closed factories. Three died in Germany, and protesters in Johannesburg burned an American flag outside the American embassy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been alleged that some of these activities were organized by the
      <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Communist Party of the Soviet Union">
       Communist Party
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At Langone Funeral Home in Boston's
      <a href="/wiki/North_End,_Boston" title="North End, Boston">
       North End
      </a>
      , more than 10,000 mourners viewed Sacco and Vanzetti in open caskets over two days. At the funeral parlor, a wreath over the caskets announced
      <i>
       Aspettando l'ora della vendetta
      </i>
      (Awaiting the hour of vengeance). On Sunday, August 28, a two-hour funeral procession bearing huge floral tributes moved through the city. Police blocked the route, which passed the State House, and at one point mourners and the police clashed. The hearses reached
      <a href="/wiki/Forest_Hills_Cemetery" title="Forest Hills Cemetery">
       Forest Hills Cemetery
      </a>
      where, after a brief eulogy, the bodies were cremated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       Boston Globe
      </i>
      called it "one of the most tremendous funerals of modern times."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Will_H._Hays" title="Will H. Hays">
       Will H. Hays
      </a>
      , head of the motion picture industry's umbrella organization, ordered all film of the funeral procession destroyed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sacco's ashes were sent to Torremaggiore, the town of his birth, where they are interred at the base of a monument erected in 1998. Vanzetti's ashes were buried with his mother in Villafalletto.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Continuing_protests_and_analyses">
       Continuing protests and analyses
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Continuing protests and analyses">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_anarchist" title="Italian anarchist">
       Italian anarchist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Severino_Di_Giovanni" title="Severino Di Giovanni">
       Severino Di Giovanni
      </a>
      , one of the most vocal supporters of Sacco and Vanzetti in Argentina, bombed the American embassy in Buenos Aires a few hours after the two men were sentenced to death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pigna_151-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pigna-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A few days after the executions, Sacco's widow thanked Di Giovanni by letter for his support and added that the director of the tobacco firm
      <i>
       Combinados
      </i>
      had offered to produce a cigarette brand named "Sacco &amp; Vanzetti".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pigna_151-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pigna-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On November 26, 1927, Di Giovanni and others bombed a Combinados tobacco shop.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pigna_151-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pigna-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On December 24, 1927, Di Giovanni blew up the headquarters of
      <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">
       The National City Bank of New York
      </a>
      and of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bank_of_Boston" title="Bank of Boston">
       Bank of Boston
      </a>
      in Buenos Aires in apparent protest of the execution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pigna_151-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pigna-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In December 1928, Di Giovanni and others failed in an attempt to bomb the train in which
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President-elect" title="President-elect">
       President-elect
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">
       Herbert Hoover
      </a>
      was traveling during his visit to Argentina.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pigna_151-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pigna-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Three months later, bombs exploded in the New York subway, in a Philadelphia church, and at the home of the mayor of Baltimore. One of the jurors in the Dedham trial had his house bombed, throwing him and his family from their beds. Less than a year after the executions, a bomb destroyed the front porch of the home of executioner Robert Elliott. As late as 1932, Judge Thayer's home was wrecked and his wife and housekeeper were injured in a bomb blast.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_152-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Afterward, Thayer lived permanently at his club in Boston, guarded 24 hours a day until his death.
     </p>
     <p>
      In October 1927,
      <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">
       H. G. Wells
      </a>
      wrote an essay that discussed the case at length. He called it "a case like the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreyfus_case" title="Dreyfus case">
       Dreyfus case
      </a>
      , by which the soul of a people is tested and displayed." He felt that Americans failed to understand what about the case roused European opinion:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_153-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wells-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       The guilt or innocence of these two Italians is not the issue that has excited the opinion of the world. Possibly they were actual murderers, and still more possibly they knew more than they would admit about the crime.... Europe is not "retrying" Sacco and Vanzetti or anything of the sort. It is saying what it thinks of Judge Thayer. Executing political opponents as political opponents after the fashion of
       <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">
        Mussolini
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalin" title="Stalin">
        Moscow
       </a>
       we can understand, or bandits as bandits; but this business of trying and executing murderers as Reds, or Reds as murderers, seems to be a new and very frightening line for the courts of a State in the most powerful and civilized Union on earth to pursue.
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      He used the case to complain that Americans were too sensitive to foreign criticism: "One can scarcely let a sentence that is not highly flattering glance across the Atlantic without some American blowing up."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wells_153-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wells-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1928,
      <a href="/wiki/Upton_Sinclair" title="Upton Sinclair">
       Upton Sinclair
      </a>
      published his novel
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Boston_(novel)" title="Boston (novel)">
        Boston
       </a>
      </i>
      , an indictment of the American judicial system. He explored Vanzetti's life and writings, as its focus, and mixed fictional characters with historical participants in the trials. Though his portrait of Vanzetti was entirely sympathetic, Sinclair disappointed advocates for the defense by failing to absolve Sacco and Vanzetti of the crimes, however much he argued that their trial had been unjust.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Years later, he explained: "Some of the things I told displeased the fanatical believers; but having portrayed the aristocrats as they were, I had to do the same thing for the anarchists."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While doing research for the book, Sinclair found evidence that Sacco and Vanzetti were guilty; he referred to this as an "ethical problem" but did not include the information in his book.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When the letters Sacco and Vanzetti wrote appeared in print in 1928, journalist
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Lippmann" title="Walter Lippmann">
       Walter Lippmann
      </a>
      commented: "If Sacco and Vanzetti were professional bandits, then historians and biographers who attempt to deduce character from personal documents might as well shut up shop. By every test that I know of for judging character, these are the letters of innocent men."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On January 3, 1929, as Gov. Fuller left the inauguration of his successor, he found a copy of the
      <i>
       Letters
      </i>
      thrust at him by someone in the crowd. He knocked it to the ground "with an exclamation of contempt."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Intellectual and literary supporters of Sacco and Vanzetti continued to speak out. In 1936, on the day when
      <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">
       Harvard
      </a>
      celebrated its 300th anniversary, 28 Harvard alumni issued a statement attacking the University's retired President Lowell for his role on the Governor's Advisory Committee in 1927. They included
      <a href="/wiki/Heywood_Broun" title="Heywood Broun">
       Heywood Broun
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Cowley" title="Malcolm Cowley">
       Malcolm Cowley
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Granville_Hicks" title="Granville Hicks">
       Granville Hicks
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Dos_Passos" title="John Dos Passos">
       John Dos Passos
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Massachusetts_judicial_reform">
       Massachusetts judicial reform
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Massachusetts judicial reform">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Following the SJC's assertion that it could not order a new trial even if there was new evidence that "would justify a different verdict," a movement for "drastic reform" quickly took shape in Boston's legal community.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytseven_101-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytseven-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In December 1927, four months after the executions, the Massachusetts Judicial Council cited the Sacco and Vanzetti case as evidence of "serious defects in our methods of administering justice." It proposed a series of changes designed to appeal to both sides of the political divide, including restrictions on the number and timing of appeals. Its principal proposal addressed the SJC's right to review. It argued that a judge would benefit from a full review of a trial, and that no one man should bear the burden in a capital case. A review could defend a judge whose decisions were challenged and make it less likely that a governor would be drawn into a case. It asked for the SJC to have right to order a new trial "upon any ground if the interests of justice appear to inquire it."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Governor Fuller endorsed the proposal in his January 1928 annual message.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
       <a href="#cite_note-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Judicial Council repeated its recommendations in 1937 and 1938. Finally, in 1939 the language it had proposed was adopted. Since that time, the SJC has been required to review all death penalty cases, to consider the entire case record, and to affirm or overturn the verdict on the law and on the evidence or "for any other reason that justice may require." (Mass laws, 1939 c 341)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
       <a href="#cite_note-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
       <a href="#cite_note-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_viewpoints">
       Historical viewpoints
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Historical viewpoints">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Many historians, especially legal historians, have concluded the Sacco and Vanzetti prosecution, trial, and aftermath constituted a blatant disregard for political
      <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">
       civil liberties
      </a>
      , and especially criticize Thayer's decision to deny a retrial.
     </p>
     <p>
      John W. Johnson has said that the authorities and jurors were influenced by strong
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Italianism" title="Anti-Italianism">
       anti-Italian prejudice
      </a>
      and the prejudice against immigrants widely held at the time, especially in New England.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JOH_167-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JOH-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Against charges of racism and racial prejudice, Paul Avrich and Brenda and James Lutz point out that both men were known anarchist members of a militant organization, members of which had been conducting a violent campaign of bombing and attempted assassinations, acts condemned by most Americans of all backgrounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LUT_169-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LUT-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though in general anarchist groups did not finance their militant activities through bank robberies, a fact noted by the investigators of the Bureau of Investigation, this was not true of the Galleanist group. Mario Buda readily told an interviewer: "
      <i>
       Andavamo a prenderli dove c'erano
      </i>
      " ("We used to go and get it [money] where it was")  meaning factories and banks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVR1_26-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-AVR1-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The guard Berardelli was also Italian.
     </p>
     <p>
      Johnson and Avrich suggest that the government prosecuted Sacco and Vanzetti for the robbery-murders as a convenient means to put a stop to their militant activities as Galleanists, whose bombing campaign at the time posed a lethal threat, both to the government and to many Americans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JOH_167-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-JOH-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
       <a href="#cite_note-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Faced with a secretive underground group whose members resisted interrogation and believed in their cause, Federal and local officials using conventional law enforcement tactics had been repeatedly stymied in their efforts to identify all members of the group or to collect enough evidence for a prosecution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LUT_169-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-LUT-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most historians believe that Sacco and Vanzetti were involved at some level in the Galleanist bombing campaign, although their precise roles have not been determined.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVR1_26-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-AVR1-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1955
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Charles_Poggi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Charles Poggi (page does not exist)">
       Charles Poggi
      </a>
      , a longtime anarchist and American citizen, traveled to
      <a href="/wiki/Savignano_sul_Rubicone" title="Savignano sul Rubicone">
       Savignano
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Emilia-Romagna" title="Emilia-Romagna">
       Emilia-Romagna
      </a>
      region of Italy to visit old comrades, including the Galleanists' principal bombmaker, Mario "Mike" Buda.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVR1_26-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-AVR1-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While discussing the South Braintree robbery, Buda told Poggi, "
      <i>
       Sacco c'era
      </i>
      " (Sacco was there).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVR1_26-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-AVR1-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Poggi added that he "had a strong feeling that Buda himself was one of the robbers, though I didn't ask him and he didn't say."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Whether Buda and Ferruccio Coacci, whose shared rental house contained the manufacturer's diagram of a .32 Savage automatic pistol (matching the .32 Savage pistol believed to have been used to shoot both Berardelli and Parmenter), had also participated in the South Braintree robbery and murders would remain a matter of speculation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Later_evidence_and_investigations">
       Later evidence and investigations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Later evidence and investigations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 1941, anarchist leader
      <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Tresca" title="Carlo Tresca">
       Carlo Tresca
      </a>
      , a member of the Sacco and Vanzetti Defense Committee, told
      <a href="/wiki/Max_Eastman" title="Max Eastman">
       Max Eastman
      </a>
      , "Sacco was guilty but Vanzetti was innocent",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEW_174-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEW-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although it is clear from his statement that Tresca equated guilt only with the act of pulling the trigger,
      <i>
       i.e.
      </i>
      , Vanzetti was not the principal triggerman in Tresca's view, but was an accomplice to Sacco. This conception of innocence is in sharp contrast to
      <a href="/wiki/Pinkerton_liability" title="Pinkerton liability">
       the legal one
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singer_1987_175-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Singer_1987-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Both
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Nation" title="The Nation">
        The Nation
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">
        The New Republic
       </a>
      </i>
      refused to publish Tresca's revelation, which Eastman said occurred after he pressed Tresca for the truth about the two men's involvement in the shooting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEW_174-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NEW-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The story finally appeared in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/National_Review" title="National Review">
        National Review
       </a>
      </i>
      in October 1961.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RUS_176-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RUS-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Others who had known Tresca confirmed that he had made similar statements to them,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RUS_176-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-RUS-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but Tresca's daughter insisted her father never hinted at Sacco's guilt. Others attributed Tresca's revelations to his disagreements with the Galleanists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Labor organizer Anthony Ramuglia, an anarchist in the 1920s, said in 1952 that a Boston anarchist group had asked him to be a false alibi witness for Sacco. After agreeing, he had remembered that he had been in jail on the day in question, so he could not testify.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Both men had previously fled to Mexico, changing their names in order to evade draft registration, a fact the prosecutor in their murder trial used to demonstrate their lack of patriotism and which they were not allowed to rebut. Sacco and Vanzetti's supporters would later argue that the men fled the country to avoid persecution and conscription; their critics said they left to escape detection and arrest for militant and seditious activities in the United States. However, a 1953 Italian history of anarchism written by anonymous colleagues revealed a different motivation:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       Several dozen Italian anarchists left the United States for Mexico. Some have suggested they did so because of cowardice. Nothing could be more false. The idea to go to Mexico arose in the minds of several comrades who were alarmed by the idea that, remaining in the United States, they would be forcibly restrained from leaving for Europe, where the revolution that had burst out in Russia that February promised to spread all over the continent.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
        <a href="#cite_note-179">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         179
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      In October 1961,
      <a href="/wiki/Ballistics" title="Ballistics">
       ballistic tests
      </a>
      were run with improved technology on Sacco's
      <a href="/wiki/Colt%27s_Manufacturing_Company" title="Colt's Manufacturing Company">
       Colt
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Semi-automatic_firearm" title="Semi-automatic firearm">
       semi-automatic
      </a>
      pistol. The results confirmed that the bullet that killed Berardelli in 1920 was fired from Sacco's pistol.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Thayer court's habit of mistakenly referring to Sacco's .32 Colt pistol as well as any other automatic pistol as a "revolver" (a popular custom of the day) has sometimes mystified later-generation researchers attempting to follow the forensic evidence trail.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COM_56-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-COM-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1987, Charlie Whipple, a former
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">
        Boston Globe
       </a>
      </i>
      editorial page editor, revealed a conversation that he had with Sergeant Edward J. Seibolt in 1937. According to Whipple, Seibolt said that "we switched the murder weapon in that case", but indicated that he would deny this if Whipple ever printed it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHI_181-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WHI-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
       <a href="#cite_note-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
