      However, at the time of the Sacco and Vanzetti trial, Seibolt was only a patrolman, and did not work in the Boston Police ballistics department; Seibolt died in 1961 without corroborating Whipple's story.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHI_181-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-WHI-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1935, Captain Charles Van Amburgh, a key ballistics witness for the prosecution, wrote a six-part article on the case for a pulp detective magazine. Van Amburgh described a scene in which Thayer caught defense ballistics expert Hamilton trying to leave the courtroom with Sacco's gun. However, Thayer said nothing about such a move during the hearing on the gun barrel switch and refused to blame either side. Following the private hearing on the gun barrel switch, Van Amburgh kept Sacco's gun in his house, where it remained until the
      <i>
       Boston Globe
      </i>
      did an expose in 1960.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sacco's .32 Colt pistol is claimed to have passed in and out of police custody and to have been dismantled several times between 1925 and 1961 for the conduct of various examinations, most notably in the demonstration performed by defense firearms expert Albert H. Hamilton in which he removed the original barrel from Sacco's .32 Colt. It has been alleged that this dismantling and the exchange of barrels between Hamilton's two .32 Colt Automatic pistols and Sacco's gun effectively interrupted the
      <a href="/wiki/Chain_of_custody" title="Chain of custody">
       chain of custody
      </a>
      of the evidence. If so, it is an example of gross misconduct on the part of the defense, and rebuts the claim that the men were framed by the prosecution. The central problem with these charges is that the evidence tying Sacco's gun to the South Braintree murders was based not only on the .32-caliber Colt pistol barrel and the .32-caliber
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      , the fatal bullet that killed Berardelli, but also the spent .32 Auto cartridge cases (shell casings) found at the scene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOD_100-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOD-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
         <span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (May 2014)">
          original research?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Hamilton acknowledged that the new .32 barrel in Sacco's Colt pistol must have come from one of Hamilton's own brand-new two .32 Colt pistols that he had used for his demonstration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOD_100-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOD-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      and one of the South Braintree .32-caliber empty shell casings were both independently linked to Sacco's Colt, a conspirator determined to fabricate evidence linking Sacco's gun to Berardelli's murder would have to access police evidence lockers and switch the barrel that fired
      <i>
       Bullet III
      </i>
      into Sacco's gun. In addition, he would have to substitute a spent .32 Auto shell casing fired from Sacco's Colt of the same type as the obsolete loading retrieved by police at the murder scene, or else obtain the actual murder weapon, then transfer that weapon's barrel and firing pin into the .32 Colt pistol taken from Sacco, all before Calvin Goddard's first microscopic examination in 1927 when the first scientific comparative match was made to Sacco's gun.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOD_100-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOD-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">
         <span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (May 2014)">
          original research?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Critics of the prosecution's evidence emphasize that while four bullets were recovered from Berardelli, the slain guard, only one bullet was linked to Sacco's pistol. The other three bullets were found to have been fired from a .32 Savage automatic pistol that was never recovered. Several witnesses insisted that one gunman fired four bullets into Berardelli, who might have been the man wielding the .32 Savage. "I seen this fellow shoot this fellow," one witness told the court. "It was the last shot. He put four bullets into him."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1927, the defense suggested that the bullet had been planted, noting the scratches on the base of the one bullet unlike those found on the others. The Lowell Commission dismissed this claim as desperate. One theory is that another gunman, perhaps Mario Buda, must have been involved along with Sacco, and that this unknown gunman used a .32 Savage pistol to shoot Parmenter twice and Berardelli three times. He left Sacco to fatally shoot Berardelli with the sixth .32 bullet traced to the latter's .32 Colt pistol.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1973 a former mobster published a confession by Frank "Butsy" Morelli, Joe's brother. "We whacked them out, we killed those guys in the robbery," Butsy Morelli told Vincent Teresa. "These two greaseballs Sacco and Vanzetti took it on the chin."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Before his death in June 1982, Giovanni Gambera, a member of the four-person team of anarchist leaders who met shortly after the arrest of Sacco and Vanzetti to plan their defense, told his son that "everyone [in the anarchist inner circle] knew that Sacco was guilty and that Vanzetti was innocent as far as the actual participation in killing."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Russell had originally written about the case, arguing that Sacco and Vanzetti were innocent, but further research led him to write a 1962 book, asserting that Sacco was guilty. Russell used the Gambera revelation as the basis of a new book in 1986, in which he claims that the case is "solved." He presented his view that Sacco was one of the shooters, while Vanzetti was an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accessory_after_the_fact" title="Accessory after the fact">
       accessory after the fact
      </a>
      . While Russell's 1962 book was praised, even by those who disagreed with his conclusion, for being balanced and well-reasoned, his 1986 book was much more negatively received. In the latter, the "accessory after the fact" legal theory is incorrect: Massachusetts law, now and at the time of the crime, allowed both men to be charged as joint principals in a robbery-homicide, for which they were convicted. From a legal standpoint, it does not matter how many shots, or even
      <i>
       if
      </i>
      , Vanzetti fired, to establish his legal culpability for the robbery and murders.
     </p>
     <p>
      Months before he died, the distinguished jurist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_E._Wyzanski,_Jr." title="Charles E. Wyzanski, Jr.">
       Charles E. Wyzanski, Jr.
      </a>
      , who had presided for 45 years on the U.S. District Court in Massachusetts, wrote to Russell stating, "I myself am persuaded by your writings that Sacco was guilty." The judge's assessment was significant, because he was one of
      <a href="/wiki/Felix_Frankfurter" title="Felix Frankfurter">
       Felix Frankfurter
      </a>
      's "Hot Dogs," and Justice Frankfurter had advocated his appointment to the federal bench.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
       <a href="#cite_note-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">
        Los Angeles Times
       </a>
      </i>
      published an article on December 24, 2005, "Sinclair Letter Turns Out to Be Another Expose", which references a newly discovered letter from Upton Sinclair to attorney John Beardsley in which Sinclair, a socialist writer famous for his muckraking novels, revealed a conversation with Fred Moore, attorney for Sacco and Vanzetti. In that conversation, in response to Sinclair's request for the truth, Moore stated that both Sacco and Vanzetti were in fact guilty, and that Moore had fabricated their alibis in an attempt to avoid a guilty verdict. Nevertheless Sinclair mentioned in the letter this episode which conflicted with his novel "Boston", where the trial is presented as a miscarriage of justice affected by racial and political prejudice. This to conform with his commitment to the utmost truth regarding their guilt or innocence. Subsequent letters indicate that, in order to avoid loss of sales to his radical readership, particularly abroad, and due to fears for his own safety, Sinclair didn't change the premise of his novel in that respect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
       <a href="#cite_note-188">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        188
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dukakis_proclamation">
       Dukakis proclamation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Dukakis proclamation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 1977, as the 50th anniversary of the executions approached, Massachusetts Governor
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dukakis" title="Michael Dukakis">
       Michael Dukakis
      </a>
      asked the Office of the Governor's Legal Counsel to report on "whether there are substantial grounds for believingat least in the light of the legal standards of todaythat Sacco and Vanzetti were unfairly convicted and executed" and to recommend appropriate action.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
       <a href="#cite_note-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The resulting "Report to the Governor in the Matter of Sacco and Vanzetti" detailed grounds for doubting that the trial was conducted fairly in the first instance, and argued as well that such doubts were only reinforced by "later-discovered or later-disclosed evidence."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Report questioned prejudicial cross-examination that the trial judge allowed, the judge's hostility, the fragmentary nature of the evidence, and eyewitness testimony that came to light after the trial. It found the judge's charge to the jury troubling for the way it emphasized the defendants' behavior at the time of their arrest and highlighted certain physical evidence that was later called into question.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
       <a href="#cite_note-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Report also dismissed the argument that the trial had been subject to judicial review, noting that "the system for reviewing murder cases at the time...failed to provide the safeguards now present."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
       <a href="#cite_note-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Based on recommendations of the Office of Legal Counsel, Dukakis declared August 23, 1977, the 50th anniversary of their execution, as Nicola Sacco and Bartolomeo Vanzetti Memorial Day.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
       <a href="#cite_note-193">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        193
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His proclamation, issued in English and Italian, stated that Sacco and Vanzetti had been unfairly tried and convicted and that "any disgrace should be forever removed from their names." He did not pardon them, because that would imply they were guilty. Neither did he assert their innocence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
       <a href="#cite_note-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icue_195-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-icue-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
       <a href="#cite_note-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A resolution to censure Governor Dukakis failed in the Massachusetts Senate by a vote of 23 to 12.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
       <a href="#cite_note-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dukakis later expressed regret only for not reaching out to the families of the victims of the crime.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
       <a href="#cite_note-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Later_tributes">
       Later tributes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Later tributes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_victims.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Memorial_to_victims.JPG/220px-Memorial_to_victims.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Memorial_to_victims.JPG/330px-Memorial_to_victims.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Memorial_to_victims.JPG/440px-Memorial_to_victims.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_to_victims.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Memorial poster for the victims, also located on the corner of French Avenue and Pearl Street in Braintree, Mass
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A memorial committee tried to present a plaster cast executed in 1937 by
      <a href="/wiki/Gutzon_Borglum" title="Gutzon Borglum">
       Gutzon Borglum
      </a>
      , the sculptor of
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rushmore" title="Mount Rushmore">
       Mount Rushmore
      </a>
      , to Massachusetts governors and Boston mayors in 1937, 1947, and 1957 without success. On August 23, 1997, on the 70th anniversary of the Sacco and Vanzetti executions, Boston's first
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian-American" title="Italian-American">
       Italian-American
      </a>
      Mayor,
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Menino" title="Thomas Menino">
       Thomas Menino
      </a>
      , and the Italian-American Governor of Massachusetts,
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cellucci" title="Paul Cellucci">
       Paul Cellucci
      </a>
      , unveiled the work at the
      <a href="/wiki/Boston_Public_Library" title="Boston Public Library">
       Boston Public Library
      </a>
      , where it remains on display.
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       The city's acceptance of this piece of artwork is not intended to reopen debate about the guilt or innocence of Sacco and Vanzetti," Menino said. "It is intended to remind us of the dangers of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miscarried_justice" title="Miscarried justice">
        miscarried justice
       </a>
       , and the right we all have to a fair trial.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
        <a href="#cite_note-199">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         199
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The event occasioned a renewed debate about the fairness of the trial in the editorial pages of the
      <i>
       Boston Herald
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
       <a href="#cite_note-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A mosaic mural portraying the trial of Sacco and Vanzetti is installed on the main campus of Syracuse University. In Braintree, Massachusetts on the Corner of French Ave and Pearl Street, a memorial marks the site of the murders. The memorial has two exhibits. The first is a weatherproof poster that discusses the crime and the subsequent trial. The second exhibit is a metal plaque that memorializes the victims of the crime.
     </p>
     <p>
      The "Sacco and Vanzetti
      <a href="/wiki/Centuria" title="Centuria">
       Centuria
      </a>
      " was an American anarchist military unit in the
      <a href="/wiki/Durruti_Column" title="Durruti Column">
       Durruti Column
      </a>
      that fought in the
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">
       Spanish Civil War
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
       <a href="#cite_note-201">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        201
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many sites in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Former_USSR" title="Former USSR">
       former USSR
      </a>
      are named after "Sacco and Vanzetti": for example, a beer production facility in
      <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">
       Moscow
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
       <a href="#cite_note-202">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        202
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      a
      <a href="/wiki/Kolkhoz" title="Kolkhoz">
       kolkhoz
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Donetsk" title="Donetsk">
       Donetsk
      </a>
      region,
      <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">
       Ukraine
      </a>
      ; and a street and an apartment complex in
      <a href="/wiki/Yekaterinburg" title="Yekaterinburg">
       Yekaterinburg
      </a>
      .;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
       <a href="#cite_note-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      'Sacco and Vanzetti' was also a popular brand of Russian pencil from 1930-2007. Numerous towns in Italy have streets named after Sacco and Vanzetti, including Via Sacco-Vanzetti in
      <a href="/wiki/Torremaggiore" title="Torremaggiore">
       Torremaggiore
      </a>
      , Sacco's home town; and
      <a href="/wiki/Villafalletto" title="Villafalletto">
       Villafalletto
      </a>
      , Vanzetti's.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
       <a href="#cite_note-204">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        204
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Plays">
       Plays
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Plays">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The plot of
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_Anderson" title="Maxwell Anderson">
        Maxwell Anderson's
       </a>
       1935 play
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Winterset_(play)" title="Winterset (play)">
         Winterset
        </a>
       </i>
       was inspired by the case.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/James_Thurber" title="James Thurber">
        James Thurber
       </a>
       and Elliot Nugent's 1940 play
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Male_Animal" title="The Male Animal">
         The Male Animal
        </a>
       </i>
       turns on a college professor's insistence on reading Vanzetti's statement at sentencing to his English composition class.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
        <a href="#cite_note-205">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         205
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was adapted as a film the next year, starring
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fonda" title="Henry Fonda">
        Henry Fonda
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Olivia_de_Havilland" title="Olivia de Havilland">
        Olivia de Havilland
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
        <a href="#cite_note-206">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         206
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2000, the play
       <i>
        Voices on the Wind
       </i>
       by
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eric_Paul_Erickson&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eric Paul Erickson (page does not exist)">
        Eric Paul Erickson
       </a>
       centers around the final hours of the lives of Sacco and Vanzetti. Former Massachusetts Governor
       <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dukakis" title="Michael Dukakis">
        Michael Dukakis
       </a>
       recorded an audio clip of his public statement on the 50th anniversary for the production.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
        <a href="#cite_note-207">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         207
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The trial of Sacco and Vanzetti is the subject of the eponymous play by
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentine
       </a>
       playwright
       <a class="extiw" href="//es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mauricio_Kartun" title="es:Mauricio Kartun">
        Mauricio Kartun
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2001,
       <a href="/wiki/Anton_Coppola" title="Anton Coppola">
        Anton Coppola
       </a>
       , uncle of
       <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola" title="Francis Ford Coppola">
        Francis Ford Coppola
       </a>
       , premiered his opera
       <i>
        Sacco and Vanzetti
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
        <a href="#cite_note-208">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         208
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2014, Joseph Silovsky wrote and performed in an Off-Broadway play about Sacco and Vanzetti,
       <i>
        Send for the Million Men.
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209">
        <a href="#cite_note-209">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         209
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Films">
       Films
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Films">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       In the 1942 film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Male_Animal" title="The Male Animal">
         The Male Animal
        </a>
       </i>
       a college professor reads a letter written by Vanzetti to his class, under the premise of free speech and free thought ignoring the threat of sanction and dismissal from the college trustee board.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sacco_e_Vanzetti" title="Sacco e Vanzetti">
         Sacco e Vanzetti
        </a>
       </i>
       , a 1971 film by Italian
       <a href="/wiki/Film_director" title="Film director">
        director
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Giuliano_Montaldo" title="Giuliano Montaldo">
        Giuliano Montaldo
       </a>
       covers the case.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210">
        <a href="#cite_note-210">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         210
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">
        Joan Baez
       </a>
       performed the song
       <a href="/wiki/Here%27s_to_You_(song)" title="Here's to You (song)">
        "Here's To You"
       </a>
       (music by
       <a href="/wiki/Ennio_Morricone" title="Ennio Morricone">
        Ennio Morricone
       </a>
       , lyrics by Baez) for the film.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sacco_and_Vanzetti_(film)" title="Sacco and Vanzetti (film)">
         Sacco and Vanzetti
        </a>
       </i>
       (2006) was a documentary film.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
        <a href="#cite_note-211">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         211
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Sacco and Vanzetti are characters in the film
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/No_God,_No_Master_(film)" title="No God, No Master (film)">
         No God, No Master
        </a>
       </i>
       (2012)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212">
        <a href="#cite_note-212">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         212
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Music">
       Music
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Music">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       In 1932, composer
       <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Crawford_Seeger" title="Ruth Crawford Seeger">
        Ruth Crawford Seeger
       </a>
       wrote the song "Sacco, Vanzetti" on commission from the Society of Contemporary Music in Philadelphia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213">
        <a href="#cite_note-213">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         213
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1960,
       <a href="/wiki/Folkways_Records" title="Folkways Records">
        Folkways Records
       </a>
       released an LP titled
       <i>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Ballads_of_Sacco_%26_Vanzetti" title="Ballads of Sacco &amp; Vanzetti">
         Ballads of Sacco &amp; Vanzetti
        </a>
       </i>
       , which had eleven songs composed and sung by folksinger
       <a href="/wiki/Woody_Guthrie" title="Woody Guthrie">
        Woody Guthrie
       </a>
       in 19461947 and one song sung by folksinger
       <a href="/wiki/Pete_Seeger" title="Pete Seeger">
        Pete Seeger
       </a>
       , using words by Nicola Sacco.
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1963,
       <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">
        Pulitzer Prize
       </a>
       -winning American
       <a href="/wiki/Composer" title="Composer">
        composer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Roger_Reynolds" title="Roger Reynolds">
        Roger Reynolds
       </a>
       set selections of Vanzetti's letters to music in the chamber work
       <i>
        Portrait of Vanzetti
       </i>
       for narrator, mixed ensemble, and electronics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214">
        <a href="#cite_note-214">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         214
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       When he died in 1964, American
       <a href="/wiki/Composer" title="Composer">
        composer
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Marc_Blitzstein" title="Marc Blitzstein">
        Marc Blitzstein
       </a>
       was working on an
       <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">
        opera
       </a>
       about Sacco and Vanzetti, which
       <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Lehrman" title="Leonard Lehrman">
        Leonard Lehrman
       </a>
       completed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
        <a href="#cite_note-215">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         215
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1971,
       <a href="/wiki/Georges_Moustaki" title="Georges Moustaki">
        Georges Moustaki
       </a>
       included his short song "Marche de Sacco et Vanzetti" on an LP album.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
        <a href="#cite_note-216">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         216
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
        <a href="#cite_note-217">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         217
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1976, the German folkgroup Manderley included the song "Sacco's Brief" (Sacco's Letter) on their album
       <i>
        Fliegt, Gedanken, fliegt
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
        <a href="#cite_note-218">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         218
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1977, folksinger
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charlie_King_(folksinger)" title="Charlie King (folksinger)">
        Charlie King
       </a>
       wrote a
       <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">
        protest song
       </a>
       called "Two Good Arms" that was based on Vanzetti's final speech.
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1988,
       <a href="/wiki/Andy_Irvine_(musician)" title="Andy Irvine (musician)">
        Andy Irvine
       </a>
       recorded "Facing the Chair" with
       <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Street" title="Patrick Street">
        Patrick Street
       </a>
       , a song about the injustice perpetrated against Sacco and Vanzetti; it was released on the album
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/No._2_Patrick_Street" title="No. 2 Patrick Street">
         No. 2 Patrick Street
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2001,
       <a href="/wiki/Against_All_Authority" title="Against All Authority">
        Against All Authority
       </a>
       released a song called Sacco and Vanzetti for their album
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nothing_New_for_Trash_Like_You" title="Nothing New for Trash Like You">
         Nothing New for Trash Like You
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2008, the metal band
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Panopticon_(band)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Panopticon (band) (page does not exist)">
        Panopticon
       </a>
       composed a song called "La Passione De Sacco &amp; Vanzetti" featuring Vanzetti's final speech excerpts. Also, the video game
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Metal_Gear_Solid_4:_Guns_of_the_Patriots" title="Metal Gear Solid 4: Guns of the Patriots">
         Metal Gear Solid 4: Guns of the Patriots
        </a>
       </i>
       featured a new arrangement of
       <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">
        Joan Baez
       </a>
       's song "
       <a href="/wiki/Here%27s_to_You_(song)" title="Here's to You (song)">
        Here's to You
       </a>
       ".
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2011, the Turkish leftist music band
       <a href="/wiki/Bandista" title="Bandista">
        Bandista
       </a>
       composed a song called "Selam size"
       <i>
        (English:
        <span lang="en" xml:lang="en">
         Salute to you
        </span>
        )
       </i>
       . Lyrics is translated from "
       <a href="/wiki/Here%27s_to_You_(song)" title="Here's to You (song)">
        Here's to You
       </a>
       " by
       <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">
        Joan Baez
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       In 2014, The game
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Metal_Gear_Solid_V:_Ground_Zeroes" title="Metal Gear Solid V: Ground Zeroes">
         Metal Gear Solid V: Ground Zeroes
        </a>
       </i>
       featured the song "
       <a href="/wiki/Here%27s_to_You_(song)" title="Here's to You (song)">
        Here's to You
       </a>
       " By
       <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">
        Joan Baez
       </a>
       playing in trailers and in-game as well.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Written_works.2C_paintings">
       Written works, paintings
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Written works, paintings">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="575" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg/220px-Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg/330px-Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg/440px-Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sacco%26Vanzetti1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Sacco and Vanzetti
        <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">
         mosaic
        </a>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Ben_Shahn" title="Ben Shahn">
         Ben Shahn
        </a>
        at
        <a href="/wiki/Syracuse_University" title="Syracuse University">
         Syracuse University
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="459" data-file-width="673" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg/220px-Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg/330px-Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg/440px-Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sacco%26Vanzetti3.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Detail of mosaic
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       In 1927, editorial cartoonist
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Ellis_(cartoonist)" title="Fred Ellis (cartoonist)">
        Fred Ellis
       </a>
       published a compendium called
       <i>
        The Case of Sacco and Vanzetti in Cartoons from the "Daily Worker"
       </i>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Upton_Sinclair" title="Upton Sinclair">
        Upton Sinclair's
       </a>
       1928 book,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Boston_(novel)" title="Boston (novel)">
         Boston: A Novel
        </a>
       </i>
       , is a fictional interpretation of the affair.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
        <a href="#cite_note-219">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         219
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">
        H. G. Wells
       </a>
       's 1928 book,
       <i>
        Mr. Blettsworthy on Rampole Island,
       </i>
       refers to the case and the main character's reaction to it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
        <a href="#cite_note-220">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         220
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In the early 1930s,
       <a href="/wiki/Ben_Shahn" title="Ben Shahn">
        Ben Shahn
       </a>
       produced a series of works related to the case, notably
       <i>
        The Passion of Sacco and Vanzetti
       </i>
       , owned by the
       <a href="/wiki/Whitney_Museum_of_American_Art" title="Whitney Museum of American Art">
        Whitney Museum of American Art
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
        New York City
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221">
        <a href="#cite_note-221">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         221
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       A similar sixty-by-twelve-foot mural by Shahn, executed in
       <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">
        marble
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vitreous_enamel" title="Vitreous enamel">
        enamel
       </a>
       , is installed on the east wall of Huntington Beard Crouse Hall at
       <a href="/wiki/Syracuse_University" title="Syracuse University">
        Syracuse University
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222">
        <a href="#cite_note-222">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         222
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The chapter 'Holding the Fort: The Night Sacco and Vanzetti Died' of
       <a href="/wiki/Frank_Moorhouse" title="Frank Moorhouse">
        Frank Moorhouse
       </a>
       's 1993 novel,
       <i>
        Grand Days
       </i>
       , depicts the violent demonstrations in Geneva following the execution.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223">
        <a href="#cite_note-223">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         223
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1935,
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell_Anderson" title="Maxwell Anderson">
        Maxwell Anderson
       </a>
       's award-winning drama
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Winterset_(play)" title="Winterset (play)">
         Winterset
        </a>
       </i>
       presented the story of a man who attempts to clear the name of his Italian immigrant father who has been executed for robbery and murder.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
        <a href="#cite_note-224">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         224
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was adapted as a
       <a href="/wiki/Winterset_(film)" title="Winterset (film)">
        feature film
       </a>
       a year later.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
        <a href="#cite_note-225">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         225
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In 1936, the third novel in
       <a href="/wiki/John_Dos_Passos" title="John Dos Passos">
        John Dos Passos
       </a>
       '
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S.A._trilogy" title="U.S.A. trilogy">
        U.S.A. trilogy
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        The Big Money
       </i>
       , Mary French works on the Sacco and Vanzetti Defense Committee and is arrested protesting their imminent executions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226">
        <a href="#cite_note-226">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         226
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/James_T._Farrell" title="James T. Farrell">
        James T. Farrell's
       </a>
       1946 novel
       <i>
        Bernard Clare
       </i>
       uses the anti-Italian sentiment provoked by coverage of the case and the crowd scene in New York City's Union Square awaiting news of the executions as critical plot elements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227">
        <a href="#cite_note-227">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         227
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Vonnegut" title="Kurt Vonnegut">
        Kurt Vonnegut's
       </a>
       1979 novel,
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jailbird_(novel)" title="Jailbird (novel)">
         Jailbird
        </a>
       </i>
       , mentions the Sacco and Vanzetti case several times before devoting two chapters to a consideration of the trial, Advisory Committee, statements made by Webster Thayer, execution, and the crucifixion parallel of executing three condemned in a single event.
      </li>
      <li>
       Mark Binelli presented the two as a
       <a href="/wiki/Laurel_and_Hardy" title="Laurel and Hardy">
        Laurel-and-Hardy
       </a>
       -like comedy team in the 2006 novel
       <i>
        Sacco And Vanzetti Must Die!
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228">
        <a href="#cite_note-228">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         228
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In one of
       <a href="/wiki/John_Sayles" title="John Sayles">
        John Sayles
       </a>
       short stories, in the collection
       <i>
        "At the Anarchist's Convention"
       </i>
       , characters discuss Dukakis pardoning Sacco and Vanzetti.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">
       Poetry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sacco_and_Vanzetti&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Poetry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/John_Dos_Passos" title="John Dos Passos">
        John Dos Passos
       </a>
       wrote the poem "They Are Dead Now," about the executions of Sacco and Vanzetti.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229">
        <a href="#cite_note-229">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         229
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       In his poem "America",
       <a href="/wiki/Allen_Ginsberg" title="Allen Ginsberg">
        Allen Ginsberg
       </a>
       presents a catalog of slogans that includes the line: "Sacco and Vanzetti must not die".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230">
        <a href="#cite_note-230">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         230
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sandburg" title="Carl Sandburg">
        Carl Sandburg
       </a>
       described the execution of Sacco and Vanzetti in his poem "Legal Midnight Hour".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231">
        <a href="#cite_note-231">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         231
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Edna_St._Vincent_Millay" title="Edna St. Vincent Millay">
        Edna St. Vincent Millay
       </a>
       wrote a poem after the executions titled "Justice Denied In Massachusetts".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232">
        <a href="#cite_note-232">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         232
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Carlos_Williams" title="William Carlos Williams">
        William Carlos Williams
       </a>
       wrote a poem entitled "Impromptu: The Suckers" in response to the trial.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233">
        <a href="#cite_note-233">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         233
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Turkish poet
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naz%C4%B1m_Hikmet" title="Nazm Hikmet">
        Nazm Hikmet
       </a>
       's poem "Sacco ile Vanzetti" (Sacco and Vanzetti) hails the two as revolutionaries.
      </li>
     </ul>

         <a class="external text" href="http://archon.brandeis.edu/index.php?p=core/search&amp;creatorid=53" rel="nofollow">
          "Jackson, Gardner"
         </a>
         , accessed February 15, 2011
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-77">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-77">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Virginia Spencer Carr,
         <i>
          Dos Passos: A Life
         </i>
         (Evanston, IL: Northwestern University Press, 1984), 222; John Dos Passos,
         <i>
          Facing the Chair: Story of Americanization of Two Foreignborn Workmen
         </i>
         (Boston: Sacco-Vanzetti Defense Committee, 1927)
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-78">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-78">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Sacco and Vanzetti,
         <i>
          Letters
         </i>
         , pp.?
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-EVA-79">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-EVA_79-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-EVA_79-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-EVA_79-2">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             c
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Evans, Colin,
         <i>
          Casebook of Forensic Detection: How Science Solved 100 of the World's Most Baffling Crimes
         </i>
         , New York: Penguin Publishers Ltd.,
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0425215598">
          ISBN 0-425-21559-8
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780425215593">
          ISBN 978-0-425-21559-3
         </a>
         (2007), pp. 12-23: "Doctor" Hamilton was not actually a doctor, but a former patent medicine salesman. He acquired a self-taught reputation as an expert firearms witness, though his testimony had been called into question as early as 1918, three years after Hamilton had testified in a New York murder case,
         <i>
          People v. Stielow
         </i>
         , that scratches on the barrel rifling of a revolver claimed to be Stielow's exactly matched marks on the bullet that killed the murder victim. Stielow was convicted and sentenced to death, and was only saved from execution after another man confessed to the murder. Subsequent new forensic examinations of both pistol and bullet demonstrated conclusively that no 'scratches' existed and that Stielow's revolver could not have been the murder weapon, and Stielow received a full pardon from the governor of New York.
        </span>

         <a class="external text" href="http://select.nytimes.com/gst/abstract.html?res=F00F1EFF395C147A93C3A81789D95F438285F9&amp;" rel="nofollow">

         </a>
         , accessed June 22, 2010. Note: Bullard had written the
         <i>

         </i>
         Pulitzer Prize-winning editorial earlier in the year. The
         <i>
          Times
         </i>
         explained that the Judicial Council was a body established by law in 1924 to recommend legal changes. It included lawyers and judges named by governor, including a judge or former judge of the SJC.
